<p><b>Marcus Aurelius Antoninus</b> (<small>Latin:&nbsp;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈmaːr.kus̠ auˈreː.li.us̠ an.toː.ˈniː.nus̠]</a></span>; English: <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɔː/: 'au' in 'fraud'">ɔː</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">aw-<span style="font-size:90%">REE</span>-lee-əs</i></a>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> 26 April 121 – 17 March 180) was <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> from 161 to 180 AD and a <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoic</a> philosopher. He was the last of the rulers known, noncontemporaneously, as the <a href="/wiki/Five_Good_Emperors" class="mw-redirect" title="Five Good Emperors">Five Good Emperors</a> and the last emperor of the <a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a>, an age of relative peace, calmness and stability for the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> lasting from 27 BC to 180 AD. He served as <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">Roman consul</a> in 140, 145, and 161. 
</p><p>Marcus Aurelius was born during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> to the emperor's nephew, the <a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetor</a> <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_(III)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcus Annius Verus (III)">Marcus Annius Verus</a>, and the heiress <a href="/wiki/Domitia_Calvilla" class="mw-redirect" title="Domitia Calvilla">Domitia Calvilla</a>. His father died when he was three, and he was raised by his mother and paternal <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_(II)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcus Annius Verus (II)">grandfather</a>. After Hadrian's <a href="/wiki/Adoption_in_ancient_Rome" title="Adoption in ancient Rome">adoptive</a> son, <a href="/wiki/Aelius_Caesar" class="mw-redirect" title="Aelius Caesar">Aelius Caesar</a>, died in 138, the emperor adopted Marcus's uncle <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> as his new heir. In turn, Antoninus adopted Marcus and Lucius, the son of Aelius. Hadrian died that year, and Antoninus became emperor. Now heir to the throne, Marcus studied Greek and Latin under tutors such as <a href="/wiki/Herodes_Atticus" title="Herodes Atticus">Herodes Atticus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Cornelius_Fronto" title="Marcus Cornelius Fronto">Marcus Cornelius Fronto</a>. He married Antoninus' daughter <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina</a> in 145.
</p><p>After Antoninus died in 161, Marcus Aurelius acceded to the throne alongside his adoptive brother, who reigned under the name <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Reign of Marcus Aurelius">his rule</a> the Roman Empire witnessed heavy military conflict. In the East, the Romans <a href="/wiki/Parthian_war_of_Lucius_Verus" class="mw-redirect" title="Parthian war of Lucius Verus">fought successfully</a> with a revitalized <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> and the rebel <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a>. Marcus defeated the <a href="/wiki/Marcomanni" title="Marcomanni">Marcomanni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quadi" title="Quadi">Quadi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sarmatian" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarmatian">Sarmatian</a> <a href="/wiki/Iazyges" title="Iazyges">Iazyges</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a>; however, these and other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a> began to represent a troubling reality for the Empire. He modified the silver purity of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman currency</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarius</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire</a> appears to have increased during his reign, but his involvement in this is unlikely since there is no record of early Christians in the 2nd century calling him a persecutor, and <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> even called Marcus a "protector of Christians".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">Antonine Plague</a> broke out in 165 or 166 and devastated the <a href="/wiki/Population_of_the_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Population of the Roman Empire">population of the Roman Empire</a>, causing the deaths of five to ten million people. Lucius Verus may have died from the plague in 169.
</p><p>Unlike some of his predecessors, Marcus chose not to adopt an heir. His children included <a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Lucilla</a>, who married Lucius, and <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>, whose succession after Marcus has been a subject of debate among both contemporary and modern historians. The <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">Column</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</a> still stand in Rome, where they were erected in celebration of his military victories. <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i>, the writings of "the philosopher" – as contemporary biographers called Marcus – are a significant source of the modern understanding of ancient Stoic philosophy. These writings have been praised by fellow writers, philosophers, monarchs, and politicians centuries after his death.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc">
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The major sources depicting the life and rule of Marcus Aurelius are patchy and frequently unreliable. The most important group of sources, the biographies contained in the <i><a href="/wiki/Augustan_History" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustan History">Historia Augusta</a></i>, claimed to be written by a group of authors at the turn of the 4th century AD, but it is believed they were in fact written by a single author (referred to here as 'the biographer') from about AD 395.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The later biographies and the biographies of subordinate emperors and usurpers are unreliable, but the earlier biographies, derived primarily from now-lost earlier sources (<a href="/wiki/Marius_Maximus" title="Marius Maximus">Marius Maximus</a> or Ignotus), are much more accurate.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_1889_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_1889-5">[5]</a></sup> For Marcus's life and rule, the biographies of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus</a>, Marcus, and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius</a> are largely reliable, but those of <a href="/wiki/Aelius_Verus" class="mw-redirect" title="Aelius Verus">Aelius Verus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avidius_Cassius" title="Avidius Cassius">Avidius Cassius</a> are not.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>A body of correspondence between Marcus's tutor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Cornelius_Fronto" title="Marcus Cornelius Fronto">Fronto</a> and various Antonine officials survives in a series of patchy manuscripts, covering the period from c. 138 to 166.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Marcus's own <i>Meditations</i> offer a window on his inner life, but are largely undateable and make few specific references to worldly affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The main narrative source for the period is <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, a Greek senator from <a href="/wiki/Bithynian" class="mw-redirect" title="Bithynian">Bithynian</a> <a href="/wiki/Nicaea" title="Nicaea">Nicaea</a> who wrote a history of Rome from its founding to 229 in eighty books. Dio is vital for the military history of the period, but his senatorial prejudices and strong opposition to imperial expansion obscure his perspective.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Some other literary sources provide specific details: the writings of the physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> on the habits of the Antonine elite, the orations of <a href="/wiki/Aelius_Aristides" title="Aelius Aristides">Aelius Aristides</a> on the temper of the times, and the constitutions preserved in the <i><a href="/wiki/Digest_(Roman_law)" title="Digest (Roman law)">Digest</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Justinianeus" class="mw-redirect" title="Codex Justinianeus">Codex Justinianeus</a></i> on Marcus' legal work.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">Inscriptions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numismatics" title="Numismatics">coin finds</a> supplement the literary sources.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Early life of Marcus Aurelius">Early life of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Marcus was born in <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> on 26 April 121. His birth name is sometimes given as Marcus Annius Verus,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> but sources assign this name to him upon his father's death and unofficial adoption by his grandfather, upon his coming of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Historia_MA_I.9–10_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historia_MA_I.9–10-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33-17">[17]</a></sup> He may have been known as Marcus Annius Catilius Severus,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> at birth or some point in his youth,<sup id="cite_ref-Historia_MA_I.9–10_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Historia_MA_I.9–10-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33-17">[17]</a></sup> or Marcus Catilius Severus Annius Verus. Upon his adoption by Antoninus as heir to the throne, he was known as Marcus Aelius Aurelius Verus Caesar and, upon his ascension, he was Marcus Aurelius Antoninus Augustus until his death;<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Epiphanius_of_Salamis" title="Epiphanius of Salamis">Epiphanius of Salamis</a>, in his chronology of the Roman emperors included in his <i><a href="/wiki/On_Weights_and_Measures" title="On Weights and Measures">On Weights and Measures</a></i>, calls him <i>Marcus Aurelius Verus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_origins">Family origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Family origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Marcus' paternal family was of Roman <a href="/wiki/Annia_gens#Anni_Verii" title="Annia gens">Italo-Hispanic origins</a>. His father was <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_(III)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcus Annius Verus (III)">Marcus Annius Verus (III)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-21">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Annia_gens" title="Annia gens">gens Annia</a> was of Italic origins (with legendary claims of descendance from <a href="/wiki/Numa_Pompilius" title="Numa Pompilius">Numa Pompilius</a>) and a branch of it, the <i>Annii Veri</i>, moved to <a href="/wiki/Espejo,_C%C3%B3rdoba" class="mw-redirect" title="Espejo, Córdoba">Ucubi</a>, a small town south east of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a> in Iberian <a href="/wiki/Baetica" class="mw-redirect" title="Baetica">Baetica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sánchez2010_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sánchez2010-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This branch rose to prominence in Rome in the late 1st century AD. Marcus's great-grandfather Marcus Annius Verus (I) was a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">senator</a> and (according to the <i>Historia Augusta</i>) ex-<a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetor</a>; his grandfather <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_(II)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcus Annius Verus (II)">Marcus Annius Verus (II)</a> was made <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a> in 73–74.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Through his grandmother <a href="/wiki/Rupilia_Faustina" class="mw-redirect" title="Rupilia Faustina">Rupilia Faustina</a>, Marcus was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Nerva-Antonine_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Nerva-Antonine dynasty">Nerva-Antonine dynasty</a>; the emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ulpia_Marciana" title="Ulpia Marciana">sororal</a> niece <a href="/wiki/Salonia_Matidia" title="Salonia Matidia">Salonia Matidia</a> was the step-mother of Rupilia and her step-sister, Hadrian's wife <a href="/wiki/Vibia_Sabina" title="Vibia Sabina">Sabina</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[note 1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marcus's mother, <a href="/wiki/Domitia_Lucilla_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Domitia Lucilla Minor">Domitia Lucilla Minor</a> (also known as Domitia Calvilla), was the daughter of the Roman patrician P. Calvisius Tullus and inherited a great fortune (described at length in one of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Younger" title="Pliny the Younger">Pliny</a>'s letters) from her parents and grandparents. Her inheritance included large brickworks on the outskirts of Rome – a profitable enterprise in an era when the city was experiencing a construction boom – and the <i>Horti Domitia Calvillae</i> (or <i>Lucillae</i>), a villa on the <a href="/wiki/Caelian_hill" class="mw-redirect" title="Caelian hill">Caelian hill</a> of Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Marcus himself was born and raised in the <i>Horti</i> and referred to the Caelian hill as 'My Caelian'.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The adoptive family of Marcus was of Roman <a href="/wiki/Aurelia_gens#Aueli_Fulvi" title="Aurelia gens">Italo-Gallic origins</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Aurelia_gens" title="Aurelia gens">gens Aurelia</a>, into which Marcus was adopted at the age of 17, was a <a href="/wiki/Sabine" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabine">Sabine</a> gens; <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a>, his adoptive father, came from the Aurelii Fulvi, a branch of the Aurelii based in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Gaul" title="Roman Gaul">Roman Gaul</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood">Childhood</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Childhood">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Marcus's sister, <a href="/wiki/Annia_Cornificia_Faustina" title="Annia Cornificia Faustina">Annia Cornificia Faustina</a>, was probably born in 122 or 123.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> His father probably died in 124, when Marcus was three years old during his praetorship.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31-38">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[note 2]</a></sup> Though he can hardly have known his father, Marcus wrote in his <i>Meditations</i> that he had learned 'modesty and manliness' from his memories of his father and the man's posthumous reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> His mother Lucilla did not remarry<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_31-38">[37]</a></sup> and, following prevailing aristocratic customs, probably did not spend much time with her son. Instead, Marcus was in the care of 'nurses',<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> and was raised after his father's death by his grandfather Marcus Annius Verus (II), who had always retained the legal authority of <i><a href="/wiki/Patria_potestas" class="mw-redirect" title="Patria potestas">patria potestas</a></i> over his son and grandson. Technically this was not an adoption, the creation of a new and different <i>patria potestas</i>. <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Catilius_Severus" title="Lucius Catilius Severus">Lucius Catilius Severus</a>, described as Marcus's maternal great-grandfather, also participated in his upbringing; he was probably the elder Domitia Lucilla's stepfather.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._33-17">[17]</a></sup> Marcus was raised in his parents' home on the <a href="/wiki/Caelian_Hill" title="Caelian Hill">Caelian Hill</a>, an upscale area with few public buildings but many aristocratic villas. Marcus's grandfather owned a palace beside the <a href="/wiki/Lateran_Palace" title="Lateran Palace">Lateran</a>, where he would spend much of his childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> Marcus thanks his grandfather for teaching him 'good character and avoidance of bad temper'.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup> He was less fond of the mistress his grandfather took and lived with after the death of his wife Rupilia.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._35_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._35-45">[43]</a></sup> Marcus was grateful that he did not have to live with her longer than he did.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Bust of a young Marcus Aurelius" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg/220px-CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg/330px-CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg/440px-CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="835" data-file-height="1088"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CapitoliniMAurelioGiovane3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bust of young Marcus Aurelius (<a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitoline Museum">Capitoline Museum</a>). <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Birley" title="Anthony Birley">Anthony Birley</a>, his modern biographer, writes of the bust: 'This is certainly a grave young man'.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>From a young age, Marcus displayed enthusiasm for <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">wrestling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a>. He trained in wrestling as a youth and into his teenage years, learned to fight in armour and joined the <a href="/wiki/Salii" title="Salii">Salii</a>, an order of  priests dedicated to the god <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a> that were responsible for the sacred shields, called <a href="/wiki/Ancile" title="Ancile">Ancilia</a>, and possibly for heralding war season's beginning and end. Marcus was educated at home, in line with contemporary aristocratic trends;<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> he thanks Catilius Severus for encouraging him to avoid public schools.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup> One of his teachers, Diognetus, a painting master, proved particularly influential; he seems to have introduced Marcus Aurelius to the philosophic way of life.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> In April 132, at the behest of Diognetus, Marcus took up the dress and habits of the philosopher: he studied while wearing a rough <a href="/wiki/Pallium_(Roman_cloak)" title="Pallium (Roman cloak)">Greek cloak</a>, and would sleep on the ground until his mother convinced him to sleep on a bed.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> A new set of tutors – the <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homeric</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Cotiaeum" title="Alexander of Cotiaeum">Alexander of Cotiaeum</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Trosius_Aper" title="Trosius Aper">Trosius Aper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuticius_Proculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Tuticius Proculus">Tuticius Proculus</a>, teachers of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[note 3]</a></sup> – took over Marcus's education in about 132 or 133.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> Marcus thanks Alexander for his training in literary styling.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup> Alexander's influence – an emphasis on matter over style and careful wording, with the occasional Homeric quotation – has been detected in Marcus' <i>Meditations</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_to_Hadrian">Succession to Hadrian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Succession to Hadrian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late 136, Hadrian almost died from a <a href="/wiki/Hemorrhage" class="mw-redirect" title="Hemorrhage">hemorrhage</a>. Convalescent in <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Villa" title="Hadrian's Villa">his villa</a> at <a href="/wiki/Tivoli,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Tivoli, Italy">Tivoli</a>, he selected Lucius Ceionius Commodus, Marcus's intended father-in-law, as his successor and <a href="/wiki/Adoption_in_ancient_Rome" title="Adoption in ancient Rome">adopted son</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> according to the biographer 'against the wishes of everyone'.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> While his motives are not certain, it would appear that his goal was to eventually place the then-too-young Marcus on the throne.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> As part of his adoption, Commodus took the name, Lucius Aelius Caesar. His health was so poor that, during a ceremony to mark his becoming heir to the throne, he was too weak to lift a large shield on his own.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup> After a brief stationing on the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a> frontier, Aelius returned to Rome to make an address to the Senate on the first day of 138. However, the night before the scheduled speech, he grew ill and died of a hemorrhage later in the day.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[note 4]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg/300px-AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="144"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AELIUS_CAESAR_RIC_II_987-671493.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Coin</a> (AD 136–138) of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> (obverse) and his adoptive son, <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Aelius" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucius Aelius">Lucius Aelius</a> (reverse). Hadrian is wearing the <a href="/wiki/Laurel_crown" class="mw-redirect" title="Laurel crown">laurel crown</a>. Inscription: HADRIANVS ... / LVCIVS CAESAR.</div></div></div>
<p>On 24 January 138, Hadrian selected Aurelius Antoninus, the husband of Marcus's aunt <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Elder" title="Faustina the Elder">Faustina the Elder</a>, as his new successor.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> As part of Hadrian's terms, Antoninus, in turn, adopted Marcus and Lucius Commodus, the son of Lucius Aelius.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> Marcus became M. Aelius Aurelius Verus, and Lucius became L. Aelius Aurelius Commodus. At Hadrian's request, Antoninus' daughter Faustina was betrothed to Lucius.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> Marcus reportedly greeted the news that Hadrian had become his adoptive grandfather with sadness, instead of joy. Only with reluctance did he move from his mother's house on the Caelian to Hadrian's private home.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>At some time in 138, Hadrian requested in the Senate that Marcus be exempt from the law barring him from becoming <i><a href="/wiki/Quaestor" title="Quaestor">quaestor</a></i> before his twenty-fourth birthday. The Senate complied, and Marcus served under Antoninus, the consul for 139.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup> Marcus's adoption diverted him from the typical career path of his class. If not for his adoption, he probably would have become <i><a href="/wiki/Triumvir_monetalis" title="Triumvir monetalis">triumvir monetalis</a></i>, a highly regarded post involving token administration of the state mint; after that, he could have served as <a href="/wiki/Military_tribune" title="Military tribune">tribune with a legion</a>, becoming the legion's nominal second-in-command. Marcus probably would have opted for travel and further education instead. As it was, Marcus was set apart from his fellow citizens. Nonetheless, his biographer attests that his character remained unaffected: 'He still showed the same respect to his relations as he had when he was an ordinary citizen, and he was as thrifty and careful of his possessions as he had been when he lived in a private household'.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>After a series of suicide attempts, all thwarted by Antoninus, Hadrian left for <a href="/wiki/Baiae" title="Baiae">Baiae</a>, a seaside resort on the <a href="/wiki/Campania" title="Campania">Campanian</a> coast. His condition did not improve, and he abandoned the diet prescribed by his doctors, indulging himself in food and drink. He sent for Antoninus, who was at his side when he died on 10 July 138.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> His remains were buried quietly at <a href="/wiki/Pozzuoli" title="Pozzuoli">Puteoli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> The succession to Antoninus was peaceful and stable: Antoninus kept Hadrian's nominees in office and appeased the senate, respecting its privileges and commuting the death sentences of men charged in Hadrian's last days.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup> For his dutiful behaviour, Antoninus was asked to accept the name 'Pius'.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Heir_to_Antoninus_Pius_.28138.E2.80.93145.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_to_Antoninus_Pius_(138–145)">Heir to Antoninus Pius (138–145)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Heir to Antoninus Pius (138–145)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_sestertius,_AD_140-144,_RIC_III_601.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Coin commemorating the betrothal of Marcus Aurelius to his eventual wife Faustina." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg/300px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg/450px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg/600px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_sestertius%2C_AD_140-144%2C_RIC_III_601.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="394"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_sestertius,_AD_140-144,_RIC_III_601.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sestertius" title="Sestertius">Sestertius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> (AD 140–144). It celebrates the betrothal of Marcus Aurelius and <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina the Younger</a> in 139, pictured below Antoninus, who is holding a statuette of <a href="/wiki/Concordia_(mythology)" title="Concordia (mythology)">Concordia</a> and clasping hands with <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Elder" title="Faustina the Elder">Faustina the Elder</a>. Inscription: ANTONINVS AVG. PIVS P. P., TR. P., CO[N]S. III / CONCORDIAE S.C.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar,_denarius,_AD_139,_RIC_III_412a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Coin of Antoninus Pius, Marcus's predecessor, depicting Antoninus on the obverse and Marcus on the reverse." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg/300px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg/450px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg/600px-Antoninus_Pius%2C_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar%2C_denarius%2C_AD_139%2C_RIC_III_412a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="389"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoninus_Pius,_with_Marcus_Aurelius_Caesar,_denarius,_AD_139,_RIC_III_412a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">Denarius</a> of Antoninus Pius (AD 139), with a portrait of Marcus Aurelius on the reverse. Inscription: ANTONINVS AVG. PIVS P. P. / AVRELIVS CAES. AVG. PII F. CO[N]S. DES.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Immediately after Hadrian's death, Antoninus approached Marcus and requested that his marriage arrangements be amended: Marcus's betrothal to <a href="/wiki/Ceionia_Fabia" title="Ceionia Fabia">Ceionia Fabia</a> would be annulled, and he would be betrothed to <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina</a>, Antoninus' daughter, instead. Faustina's betrothal to Ceionia's brother Lucius Commodus would also have to be annulled. Marcus consented to Antoninus's proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> He was made <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consul</a> for 140 with Antoninus as his colleague, and was appointed as a <i>seviri</i>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Equites" title="Equites">knights</a>' six commanders, at the order's annual parade on 15 July 139. As the heir apparent, Marcus became <i>princeps iuventutis</i>, head of the equestrian order. He now took the name Marcus Aelius Aurelius Verus Caesar.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup> Marcus would later caution himself against taking the name too seriously: 'See that you do not turn into a Caesar; do not be dipped into the <a href="/wiki/Tyrian_purple" title="Tyrian purple">purple dye</a> – for that can happen'.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup> At the senate's request, Marcus joined all the priestly colleges (<i><a href="/wiki/Pontiff" title="Pontiff">pontifices</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Augur" title="Augur">augures</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Quindecimviri_sacris_faciundis" title="Quindecimviri sacris faciundis">quindecimviri sacris faciundis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Epulones" title="Epulones">septemviri epulonum</a></i>, etc.);<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">[76]</a></sup> direct evidence for membership, however, is available only for the <a href="/wiki/Arval_Brethren" title="Arval Brethren">Arval Brethren</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antoninus demanded that Marcus reside in the House of Tiberius, the imperial palace on the Palatine, and take up the habits of his new station, the <i>aulicum fastigium</i> or 'pomp of the court', against Marcus' objections.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">[76]</a></sup> Marcus would struggle to reconcile the life of the court with his philosophic yearnings. He told himself it was an attainable goal – 'Where life is possible, then it is possible to live the right life; life is possible in a palace, so it is possible to live the right life in a palace'<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[78]</a></sup> – but he found it difficult nonetheless. He would criticize himself in the <i>Meditations</i> for 'abusing court life' in front of company.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>As quaestor, Marcus would have had little real administrative work to do. He would read imperial letters to the senate when Antoninus was absent and would do secretarial work for the senators.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup> But he felt drowned in paperwork and complained to his tutor, Marcus Cornelius Fronto: 'I am so out of breath from dictating nearly thirty letters'.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup> He was being 'fitted for ruling the state', in the words of his biographer.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> He was required to make a speech to the assembled senators as well, making oratorical training essential for the job.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89-87">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 January 145, Marcus was made consul a second time. Fronto urged him in a letter to have plenty of sleep 'so that you may come into the Senate with a good colour and read your speech with a strong voice'.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup> Marcus had complained of an illness in an earlier letter: 'As far as my strength is concerned, I am beginning to get it back; and there is no trace of the pain in my chest. But that ulcer [...]<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[note 5]</a></sup> I am having treatment and taking care not to do anything that interferes with it'.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup> Never particularly healthy or strong, Marcus was praised by Cassius Dio, writing of his later years, for behaving dutifully in spite of his various illnesses.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[86]</a></sup> In April 145, Marcus married Faustina, legally his sister, as had been planned since 138.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[87]</a></sup> Little is specifically known of the ceremony, but the biographer calls it 'noteworthy'.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[88]</a></sup> Coins were issued with the heads of the couple, and Antoninus, as <i><a href="/wiki/Pontifex_Maximus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontifex Maximus">Pontifex Maximus</a></i>, would have officiated. Marcus makes no apparent reference to the marriage in his surviving letters, and only sparing references to Faustina.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fronto_and_further_education">Fronto and further education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Fronto and further education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After taking the <i><a href="/wiki/Toga_virilis" class="mw-redirect" title="Toga virilis">toga virilis</a></i> in 136, Marcus probably began his training in <a href="/wiki/Eloquence" title="Eloquence">oratory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup> He had three tutors in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> (Aninus Macer, Caninius Celer, and <a href="/wiki/Herodes_Atticus" title="Herodes Atticus">Herodes Atticus</a>) and one in Latin (<a href="/wiki/Marcus_Cornelius_Fronto" title="Marcus Cornelius Fronto">Marcus Cornelius Fronto</a>). The latter two were the most esteemed orators of their time,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[91]</a></sup> but probably did not become his tutors until his adoption by Antoninus in 138. The preponderance of Greek tutors indicates the importance of the Greek language to the aristocracy of Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[92]</a></sup> This was the age of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sophistic" title="Second Sophistic">Second Sophistic</a>, a renaissance in Greek letters. Although educated in Rome, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i> Marcus would write his inmost thoughts in Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Atticus was controversial: an enormously rich Athenian (probably the richest man in the eastern half of the empire), he was quick to anger and resented by his fellow Athenians for his patronizing manner.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> Atticus was an inveterate opponent of <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a> and philosophic pretensions.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup> He thought the Stoics' desire for <a href="/wiki/Apatheia" title="Apatheia">apatheia</a> was foolish: they would live a 'sluggish, enervated life', he said.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup> In spite of the influence of Atticus, Marcus would later become a Stoic. He would not mention Herodes at all in his <i>Meditations</i>, in spite of the fact that they would come into contact many times over the following decades.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fronto was highly esteemed: in the self-consciously antiquarian world of Latin letters,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup> he was thought of as second only to <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, perhaps even an alternative to him.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-104">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[note 6]</a></sup> He did not care much for Atticus, though Marcus was eventually to put the pair on speaking terms. Fronto exercised a complete mastery of Latin, capable of tracing expressions through the literature, producing obscure <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonyms</a>, and challenging minor improprieties in word choice.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-104">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>A significant amount of the correspondence between Fronto and Marcus has survived.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69-109">[103]</a></sup> The pair were very close, using intimate language such as 'Farewell my Fronto, wherever you are, my most sweet love and delight. How is it between you and me? I love you and you are not here' in their correspondence.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup> Marcus spent time with Fronto's wife and daughter, both named Cratia, and they enjoyed light conversation.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>He wrote Fronto a letter on his birthday, claiming to love him as he loved himself, and calling on the gods to ensure that every word he learnt of literature, he would learn 'from the lips of Fronto'.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> His prayers for Fronto's health were more than conventional, because Fronto was frequently ill; at times, he seems to be an almost constant invalid, always suffering<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> – about one-quarter of the surviving letters deal with the man's sicknesses.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup> Marcus asks that Fronto's pain be inflicted on himself, 'of my own accord with every kind of discomfort'.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fronto never became Marcus's full-time teacher and continued his career as an advocate. One notorious case brought him into conflict with Atticus.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup> Marcus pleaded with Fronto, first with 'advice', then as a 'favour', not to attack Atticus; he had already asked Atticus to refrain from making the first blows.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup> Fronto replied that he was surprised to discover Marcus counted Atticus as a friend (perhaps Atticus was not yet Marcus' tutor), and allowed that Marcus might be correct,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup> but nonetheless affirmed his intent to win the case by any means necessary: '[T]he charges are frightful and must be spoken of as frightful. Those in particular that refer to the beating and robbing I will describe so that they savour of gall and bile. If I happen to call him an uneducated little Greek it will not mean war to the death'.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup> The outcome of the trial is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the age of twenty-five (between April 146 and April 147), Marcus had grown disaffected with his studies in <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>, and showed some signs of general <a href="/wiki/Malaise" title="Malaise">malaise</a>. His master, he writes to Fronto, was an unpleasant blowhard, and had made 'a hit at' him: 'It is easy to sit yawning next to a judge, he says, but to <i>be</i> a judge is noble work'.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> Marcus had grown tired of his exercises, of taking positions in imaginary debates. When he criticized the insincerity of conventional language, Fronto took to defend it.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup> In any case, Marcus' formal education was now over. He had kept his teachers on good terms, following them devotedly. It 'affected his health adversely', his biographer writes, to have devoted so much effort to his studies. It was the only thing the biographer could find fault with in Marcus's entire boyhood.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fronto had warned Marcus against the study of philosophy early on: "It is better never to have touched the teaching of philosophy [...] than to have tasted it superficially, with the edge of the lips, as the saying is".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> He disdained philosophy and philosophers and looked down on Marcus's sessions with <a href="/wiki/Apollonius_of_Chalcedon" title="Apollonius of Chalcedon">Apollonius of Chalcedon</a> and others in this circle.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._69-109">[103]</a></sup> Fronto put an uncharitable interpretation of Marcus's 'conversion to philosophy': 'In the fashion of the young, tired of boring work', Marcus had turned to philosophy to escape the constant exercises of oratorical training.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup> Marcus kept in close touch with Fronto, but would ignore Fronto's scruples.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apollonius may have introduced Marcus to Stoic philosophy, but <a href="/wiki/Junius_Rusticus" title="Junius Rusticus">Quintus Junius Rusticus</a> would have the strongest influence on the boy.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[note 7]</a></sup> He was the man Fronto recognized as having 'wooed Marcus away' from oratory.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[123]</a></sup> He was older than Fronto and twenty years older than Marcus. As the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Arulenus_Rusticus" title="Arulenus Rusticus">Arulenus Rusticus</a>, one of the martyrs to the tyranny of <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a> (<i>r</i>. 81–96), he was heir to the tradition of '<a href="/wiki/Stoic_Opposition" title="Stoic Opposition">Stoic Opposition</a>' to the 'bad emperors' of the 1st century;<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[124]</a></sup> the true successor of <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a> (as opposed to Fronto, the false one).<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[125]</a></sup> Marcus thanks Rusticus for teaching him 'not to be led astray into enthusiasm for rhetoric, for writing on speculative themes, for discoursing on moralizing texts.... To avoid oratory, poetry, and 'fine writing''.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philostratus" title="Philostratus">Philostratus</a> describes how even when Marcus was an old man, in the latter part of his reign, he studied under <a href="/wiki/Sextus_of_Chaeronea" title="Sextus of Chaeronea">Sextus of Chaeronea</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The Emperor Marcus was an eager disciple of Sextus the <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotian</a> philosopher, being often in his company and frequenting his house. Lucius, who had just come to Rome, asked the Emperor, whom he met on his way, where he was going to and on what errand, and Marcus answered, ' it is good even for an old man to learn; I am now on my way to Sextus the philosopher to learn what I do not yet know.' And Lucius, raising his hand to heaven, said, ' O Zeus, the king of the Romans in his old age takes up his <a href="/wiki/Wax_tablet" title="Wax tablet">tablets</a> and goes to school.'<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Births_and_deaths">Births and deaths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Births and deaths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 30 November 147, Faustina gave birth to a girl named Domitia Faustina. She was the first of at least thirteen children (including two sets of twins) that Faustina would bear over the next twenty-three years. The next day, 1 December, Antoninus gave Marcus the <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribunician</a> power and the <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium" title="Imperium">imperium</a></i> – authority over the armies and provinces of the emperor. As tribune, he had the right to bring one measure before the senate after the four Antoninus could introduce. His tribunician powers would be renewed with Antoninus's on 10 December 147.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-135">[128]</a></sup> The first mention of Domitia in Marcus's letters reveals her as a sickly infant. 'Caesar to Fronto. If the gods are willing we seem to have a hope of recovery. The diarrhea has stopped, the little attacks of fever have been driven away. But the emaciation is still extreme and there is still quite a bit of coughing'. He and Faustina, Marcus wrote, had been 'pretty occupied' with the girl's care.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[129]</a></sup> Domitia would die in 151.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Mausoleum of Hadrian" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg/220px-RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg/330px-RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg/440px-RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1491" data-file-height="945"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RomaCastelSantAngelo-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_of_Hadrian" class="mw-redirect" title="Mausoleum of Hadrian">Mausoleum of Hadrian</a>, where the children of Marcus and Faustina were buried</div></div></div>
<p>In 149, Faustina gave birth again, to twin sons. Contemporary coinage commemorates the event, with crossed cornucopiae beneath portrait busts of the two small boys, and the legend <i>temporum felicitas</i>, 'the happiness of the times'. They did not survive long. Before the end of the year, another family coin was issued: it shows only a tiny girl, Domitia Faustina, and one boy baby. Then another: the girl alone. The infants were buried in the <a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant'Angelo">Mausoleum of Hadrian</a>, where their epitaphs survive. They were called Titus Aurelius Antoninus and Tiberius Aelius Aurelius.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._106–07_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._106–07-138">[131]</a></sup> Marcus steadied himself: 'One man prays: 'How I may not lose my little child', but you must pray: 'How I may not be afraid to lose him'.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup> He quoted from the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> what he called the "briefest and most familiar saying [...] enough to dispel sorrow and fear":<sup id="cite_ref-Meditations_x_pp._78,_224_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meditations_x_pp._78,_224-140">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><div class="poem">
<p>&nbsp;leaves,<br>
the wind scatters some on the face of the ground;<br>
like unto them are the children of men.
</p>
</div><p>
–&nbsp;<i>Iliad</i> vi.146<sup id="cite_ref-Meditations_x_pp._78,_224_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meditations_x_pp._78,_224-140">[133]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Another daughter was born on 7 March 150, <a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Annia Aurelia Galeria Lucilla</a>. At some time between 155 and 161, probably soon after 155, Marcus's mother Domitia Lucilla died.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_107_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_107-141">[134]</a></sup> Faustina probably had another daughter in 151, but the child, <a href="/wiki/Annia_Galeria_Aurelia_Faustina" title="Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina">Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina</a>, might not have been born until 153.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[135]</a></sup> Another son, Tiberius Aelius Antoninus, was born in 152. A coin issue celebrates <i>fecunditati Augustae</i>, 'to Augusta's fertility', depicting two girls and an infant. The boy did not survive long, as evidenced by coins from 156, only depicting the two girls. He might have died in 152, the same year as Marcus's sister Cornificia.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108-143">[136]</a></sup> By 28 March 158, when Marcus replied, another of his children was dead. Marcus thanked the temple synod, 'even though this turned out otherwise'. The child's name is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[137]</a></sup> In 159 and 160, Faustina gave birth to daughters: Fadilla and Cornificia, named respectively after Faustina's and Marcus's dead sisters.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114-145">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Antoninus_Pius.27s_last_years"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Antoninus_Pius's_last_years">Antoninus Pius's last years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Antoninus Pius's last years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg/220px-Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg/330px-Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg/440px-Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2300" data-file-height="3066"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperator_Antoninus_Pius.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Lucius started his political career as a quaestor in 153. He was consul in 154,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[139]</a></sup> and was consul again with Marcus in 161.<sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup> Lucius had no other titles, except that of 'son of Augustus'. Lucius had a markedly different personality from Marcus: he enjoyed sports of all kinds, but especially hunting and wrestling; he took obvious pleasure in the circus games and gladiatorial fights.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[141]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[note 8]</a></sup> He did not marry until 164.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 156, Antoninus turned 70. He found it difficult to keep himself upright without <a href="/wiki/Corset" title="Corset">stays</a>. He started nibbling on dry bread to give him the strength to stay awake through his morning receptions. As Antoninus aged, Marcus would take on more administrative duties, more still when he became the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">praetorian prefect</a> (an office that was as much secretarial as military) when Marcus Gavius Maximus died in 156 or 157.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[146]</a></sup> In 160, Marcus and Lucius were designated joint consuls for the following year. Antoninus may have already been ill.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114-145">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two days before his death, the biographer reports, Antoninus was at his ancestral estate at <a href="/wiki/Lorium" title="Lorium">Lorium</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Etruria" title="Etruria">Etruria</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[147]</a></sup> about 19 kilometres (12&nbsp;mi) from Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-Victor,_15:7_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Victor,_15:7-156">[148]</a></sup> He ate Alpine cheese at dinner quite greedily. In the night he vomited; he had a fever the next day. The day after that, 7 March 161,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup> he summoned the imperial council, and passed the state and his daughter to Marcus. The emperor gave the keynote to his life in the last word that he uttered when the tribune of the night-watch came to ask the password – 'aequanimitas' (equanimity).<sup id="cite_ref-Bury_532_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bury_532-158">[150]</a></sup> He then turned over, as if going to sleep, and died.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup> His death closed out the longest reign since Augustus, surpassing <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> by a couple of months.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emperor">Emperor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Emperor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Reign of Marcus Aurelius">Reign of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>
<h3><span id="Accession_of_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus_.28161.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession_of_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus_(161)">Accession of Marcus Aurelius and Lucius Verus (161)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Accession of Marcus Aurelius and Lucius Verus (161)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus,_British_Museum_(23455313842).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Busts of Marcus Aurelius and his co-ruler Lucius Verus" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg/220px-Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="318" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg/330px-Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg/440px-Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus%2C_British_Museum_%2823455313842%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3190" data-file-height="4613"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Co-emperors_Marcus_Aurelius_and_Lucius_Verus,_British_Museum_(23455313842).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Busts of the co-emperors Marcus Aurelius (left) and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a> (right), <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>After Antoninus died in 161, Marcus was effectively sole ruler of the Empire. The formalities of the position would follow. The Senate would soon grant him the name Augustus and the title <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i>, and he would soon be formally elected as <i><a href="/wiki/Pontifex_maximus" title="Pontifex maximus">pontifex maximus</a></i>, chief priest of the official cults. Marcus made some show of resistance: the biographer writes that he was 'compelled' to take imperial power.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[153]</a></sup> This may have been a genuine <i>horror imperii</i>, 'fear of imperial power'. Marcus, with his preference for the philosophic life, found the imperial office unappealing. His training as a <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoic</a> however, had made the choice clear to him that it was his duty.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although Marcus showed no personal affection for <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> (significantly, he does not thank him in the first book of his <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i>), he presumably believed it his duty to enact the man's succession plans.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[155]</a></sup> Thus, although the Senate planned to confirm Marcus alone, he refused to take office unless Lucius received equal powers.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[156]</a></sup> The Senate accepted, granting Lucius the <i>imperium</i>, the tribunician power, and the title <a href="/wiki/Augustus_(title)" title="Augustus (title)">Augustus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[157]</a></sup> Marcus became, in official titulature, Imperator Caesar Marcus Aurelius Antoninus Augustus; Lucius, forgoing his name Commodus and taking Marcus's family name Verus, became Imperator Caesar Lucius Aurelius Verus Augustus.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[note 9]</a></sup> It was the first time that Rome was ruled by two emperors.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-169">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[note 10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In spite of their nominal equality, Marcus held more <i><a href="/wiki/Auctoritas" title="Auctoritas">auctoritas</a></i>, or 'authority', than Lucius. He had been consul once more than Lucius, he had shared in Antoninus's rule, and he alone was <i>pontifex maximus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[162]</a></sup> It would have been clear to the public which emperor was the more senior.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_169-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-169">[161]</a></sup> As the biographer wrote: "Verus obeyed Marcus [...] as a lieutenant obeys a proconsul or a governor obeys the emperor".<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Immediately after their Senate confirmation, the emperors proceeded to the <a href="/wiki/Castra_Praetoria" title="Castra Praetoria">Castra Praetoria</a>, the camp of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a>. Lucius addressed the assembled troops, which then acclaimed the pair as <i>imperatores</i>. Then, like every new emperor since <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a>, Lucius promised the troops a special <i><a href="/wiki/Donativum" title="Donativum">donativum</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[164]</a></sup> This <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/donative" class="extiw" title="wikt:donative">donative</a>, however, was twice the size of those past: 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Sestertius" title="Sestertius">sesterces</a> (5,000 <a href="/wiki/Denarius" title="Denarius">denarii</a>) per capita, with more to officers. In return for this bounty, equivalent to several years' pay, the troops swore an oath to protect the emperors.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[165]</a></sup> The ceremony was perhaps not entirely necessary, given that Marcus's accession had been peaceful and unopposed, but it was good insurance against later military troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118-176">[166]</a></sup> Upon his accession he also devalued the <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman currency</a>. He decreased the silver purity of the denarius from 83.5% to 79% – the silver weight dropping from 2.68&nbsp;g (0.095&nbsp;oz) to 2.57&nbsp;g (0.091&nbsp;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-TulaneUniversity_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TulaneUniversity-177">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Antoninus's funeral ceremonies were, in the words of the biographer, 'elaborate'.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[168]</a></sup> If his funeral followed those of his predecessors, his body would have been cremated on a pyre at the <a href="/wiki/Campus_Martius" title="Campus Martius">Campus Martius</a>, and his spirit would have been seen as ascending to the gods' home in the heavens. Marcus and Lucius nominated their father for deification. In contrast to their behaviour during Antoninus's campaign to deify Hadrian, the Senate did not oppose the emperors' wishes. A <i><a href="/wiki/Flamen" title="Flamen">flamen</a></i>, or cultic priest, was appointed to minister the cult of the deified Divus Antoninus. Antoninus's remains were laid to rest in <a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant'Angelo">Hadrian's mausoleum</a>, beside the remains of Marcus's children and of Hadrian himself.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[169]</a></sup> The temple he had dedicated to his wife, Diva Faustina, became the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Antoninus_and_Faustina" title="Temple of Antoninus and Faustina">Temple of Antoninus and Faustina</a>. It survives as the church of San Lorenzo in Miranda.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._118-176">[166]</a></sup>
</p><p>In accordance with his will, Antoninus's fortune passed on to Faustina.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[170]</a></sup> (Marcus had little need of his wife's fortune. Indeed, at his accession, Marcus transferred part of his mother's estate to his nephew, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Ummidius_Quadratus_Annianus" title="Marcus Ummidius Quadratus Annianus">Ummius Quadratus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[171]</a></sup>) Faustina was three months pregnant at her husband's accession. During the pregnancy she dreamed of giving birth to two serpents, one fiercer than the other.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[172]</a></sup> On 31 August, she gave birth at <a href="/wiki/Lanuvium" title="Lanuvium">Lanuvium</a> to twins: T. Aurelius Fulvus Antoninus and Lucius Aurelius Commodus.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[note 11]</a></sup> Aside from the fact that the twins shared <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a>'s birthday, the omens were favorable, and the astrologers drew positive horoscopes for the children.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[175]</a></sup> The births were celebrated on the imperial coinage.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_rule">Early rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Early rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soon after the emperor's accession, Marcus's eleven-year-old daughter, Annia Lucilla, was betrothed to Lucius (in spite of the fact that he was, formally, her uncle).<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup> At the ceremonies commemorating the event, new provisions were made for the support of poor children, along the lines of earlier imperial foundations.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[178]</a></sup> Marcus and Lucius proved popular with the people of Rome, who strongly approved of their <i>civiliter</i> ("lacking pomp") behaviour. The emperors permitted free speech, evidenced by the fact that the comedy writer Marullus was able to criticize them without suffering retribution. As the biographer wrote, "No one missed the lenient ways of Pius".<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marcus replaced a number of the empire's major officials. The <i><a href="/wiki/Ab_epistulis" title="Ab epistulis">ab epistulis</a></i> Sextus Caecilius Crescens Volusianus, in charge of the imperial correspondence, was replaced with Titus Varius Clemens. Clemens was from the frontier province of <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a> and had served in the war in <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>. Recently, he had served as procurator of five provinces. He was a man suited for a time of military crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[180]</a></sup> Lucius Volusius Maecianus, Marcus's former tutor, had been <a href="/wiki/Augustal_prefect" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustal prefect">prefectural governor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Egypt</a> at Marcus's accession. Maecianus was recalled, made senator, and appointed prefect of the treasury (<i><a href="/wiki/Aerarium_Saturni" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerarium Saturni">aerarium Saturni</a></i>). He was made consul soon after.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[181]</a></sup> Fronto's son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Aufidius_Victorinus" title="Gaius Aufidius Victorinus">Gaius Aufidius Victorinus</a>, was appointed governor of <a href="/wiki/Germania_Superior" title="Germania Superior">Germania Superior</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HA_Marcus_8_1985_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HA_Marcus_8_1985-193">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fronto returned to his Roman townhouse at dawn on 28 March, having left his home in <a href="/wiki/Cirta" title="Cirta">Cirta</a> as soon as news of his pupils' accession reached him. He sent a note to the imperial freedman Charilas, asking if he could call on the emperors. Fronto would later explain that he had not dared to write the emperors directly.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[183]</a></sup> The tutor was immensely proud of his students. Reflecting on the speech he had written on taking his consulship in 143, when he had praised the young Marcus, Fronto was ebullient: "There was then an outstanding natural ability in you; there is now perfected excellence. There was then a crop of growing corn; there is now a ripe, gathered harvest. What I was hoping for then, I have now. The hope has become a reality".<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[184]</a></sup> Fronto called on Marcus alone; neither thought to invite Lucius.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120-196">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lucius was less esteemed by Fronto than his brother, as his interests were on a lower level. Lucius asked Fronto to adjudicate in a dispute he and his friend Calpurnius were having on the relative merits of two actors.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[186]</a></sup> Marcus told Fronto of his reading – <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Coelius_Antipater" title="Lucius Coelius Antipater">Coelius</a> and a little Cicero – and his family. His daughters were in Rome with their great-great-aunt Matidia; Marcus thought the evening air of the country was too cold for them. He asked Fronto for 'some particularly eloquent reading matter, something of your own, or Cato, or Cicero, or Sallust or Gracchus – or some poet, for I need distraction, especially in this kind of way, by reading something that will uplift and diffuse my pressing anxieties.'<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[187]</a></sup> Marcus's early reign proceeded smoothly; he was able to give himself wholly to philosophy and the pursuit of popular affection.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[188]</a></sup> Soon, however, he would find he had many anxieties. It would mean the end of the <i>felicitas temporum</i> ('happy times') that the coinage of 161 had proclaimed.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[189]</a></sup>
</p>

data-file-height="1944"></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:244px;max-width:244px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:181px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Piena_del_Tevere_-_Tiber_in_flood_-_Ponte_Sisto_-_Rome,_Italy_-_12_Dec._2008.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Tiber Island in flood, December 2008" 1.5x,
