2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112"></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Tiber_Island" title="Tiber Island">Tiber Island</a> seen at a forty-year high-water mark of the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>, December 2008</div></div></div></div>
<p>In either autumn 161 or spring 162,<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[note 12]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a> overflowed its banks, flooding much of Rome. It drowned many animals, leaving the city in famine. Marcus and Lucius gave the crisis their personal attention.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[note 13]</a></sup> In other times of famine, the emperors are said to have provided for the Italian communities out of the Roman granaries.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fronto's letters continued through Marcus's early reign. Fronto felt that, because of Marcus's prominence and public duties, lessons were more important now than they had ever been before. He believed Marcus was 'beginning to feel the wish to be eloquent once more, in spite of having for a time lost interest in eloquence'.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[194]</a></sup> Fronto would again remind his pupil of the tension between his role and his philosophic pretensions: 'Suppose, Caesar, that you can attain to the wisdom of <a href="/wiki/Cleanthes" title="Cleanthes">Cleanthes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Citium" title="Zeno of Citium">Zeno</a>, yet, against your will, not the philosopher's woolen cape'.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early days of Marcus's reign were the happiest of Fronto's life: Marcus was beloved by the people of Rome, an excellent emperor, a fond pupil, and perhaps most importantly, as eloquent as could be wished.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[196]</a></sup> Marcus had displayed rhetorical skill in his speech to the senate after an earthquake at <a href="/wiki/Cyzicus" title="Cyzicus">Cyzicus</a>. It had conveyed the drama of the disaster, and the Senate had been awed: "Not more suddenly or violently was the city stirred by the earthquake than the minds of your hearers by your speech". Fronto was hugely pleased.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="War_with_Parthia_.28161.E2.80.93166.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Parthia_(161–166)">War with Parthia (161–166)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: War with Parthia (161–166)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Parthian_War_of_161%E2%80%93166" title="Roman–Parthian War of 161–166">Roman–Parthian War of 161–166</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="Roman–Persian Wars">Roman–Persian Wars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:VologasesIV.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/VologasesIV.jpg/300px-VologasesIV.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2b/VologasesIV.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="145"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:VologasesIV.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Coin of <a href="/wiki/Vologases_IV_of_Parthia" class="mw-redirect" title="Vologases IV of Parthia">Vologases IV of Parthia</a>. Inscription: above ΒΑΣΙΛΕΩΣ ΒΑΣΙΛΕΩΝ ΔΟΥ, right ΑΡΣΑΚΟΥ ΒΟΛΑΓΑΣΟΥ, left ΕΠΙΦΑΝΟΥΣ ΦΙΛΕΛΛΗΝΟΣ, below ΔΙΟΥ (Greek inscription for KING OF KINGS – ARSAKIS VOLAGASES – ILLUSTRIUS PHILELLENE). Year ΔΟΥ = ΥΟΔ΄ = 474 = 162–163.</div></div></div>
<p>On his deathbed, Antoninus spoke of nothing but the state and the foreign kings who had wronged him.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[198]</a></sup> One of those kings, <a href="/wiki/Vologases_IV_of_Parthia" class="mw-redirect" title="Vologases IV of Parthia">Vologases IV of Parthia</a>, made his move in late summer or early autumn 161.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[199]</a></sup> Vologases entered the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Kingdom of Armenia</a> (then a Roman client state), expelled its king and installed his own – <a href="/wiki/Pacorus_of_Armenia" title="Pacorus of Armenia">Pacorus</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Arsacid_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Arsacid Empire">Arsacid</a> like himself.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[200]</a></sup> The governor of Cappadocia, the frontline in all Armenian conflicts, was <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Sedatius_Severianus" title="Marcus Sedatius Severianus">Marcus Sedatius Severianus</a>, a Gaul with much experience in military matters.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>Convinced by the prophet <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Abonoteichus" title="Alexander of Abonoteichus">Alexander of Abonoteichus</a> that he could defeat the Parthians easily and win glory for himself,<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[202]</a></sup> Severianus led a legion (perhaps the <a href="/wiki/Legio_IX_Hispana" title="Legio IX Hispana">IX Hispana</a><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[203]</a></sup>) into Armenia, but was trapped by the great Parthian general <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chosrhoes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Chosrhoes (page does not exist)">Chosrhoes</a> at <a href="/wiki/Elegeia" title="Elegeia">Elegeia</a>, a town just beyond the Cappadocian frontiers, high up past the headwaters of the Euphrates. After Severianus made some unsuccessful efforts to engage Chosrhoes, he committed suicide, and his legion was massacred. The campaign had lasted only three days.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>There was threat of war on other frontiers as well – in Britain, and in <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a> and Upper Germany, where the <a href="/wiki/Chatti" title="Chatti">Chatti</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Taunus" title="Taunus">Taunus</a> mountains had recently crossed over the <i><a href="/wiki/Limes_(Roman_Empire)" title="Limes (Roman Empire)">limes</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[205]</a></sup> Marcus was unprepared. Antoninus seems to have given him no military experience; the biographer writes that Marcus spent the whole of Antoninus's twenty-three-year reign at his emperor's side and not in the provinces, where most previous emperors had spent their early careers.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[note 14]</a></sup>
</p><p>More bad news arrived: the Syrian governor's army had been defeated by the Parthians, and retreated in disarray.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[208]</a></sup> Reinforcements were dispatched for the Parthian frontier. P. Julius Geminius Marcianus, an African senator commanding <a href="/wiki/Legio_X_Gemina" title="Legio X Gemina">X Gemina</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vindobona" title="Vindobona">Vindobona</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>), left for Cappadocia with detachments from the Danubian legions.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[209]</a></sup> Three full legions were also sent east: <a href="/wiki/Legio_I_Minervia" title="Legio I Minervia">I Minervia</a> from Bonn in Upper Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[210]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Adiutrix" title="Legio II Adiutrix">II Adiutrix</a> from Aquincum,<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[211]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Legio_V_Macedonica" title="Legio V Macedonica">V Macedonica</a> from Troesmis.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern frontiers were strategically weakened; frontier governors were told to avoid conflict wherever possible.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[213]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Libo_(consul_161)" title="Marcus Annius Libo (consul 161)">M. Annius Libo</a>, Marcus's first cousin, was sent to replace the Syrian governor. His first consulship was in 161, so he was probably in his early thirties,<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[214]</a></sup> and as a patrician, he lacked military experience. Marcus had chosen a reliable man rather than a talented one.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_166,_RIC_III_160.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Coin of Marcus Aurelius. Victoria appears on the reverse, commemorating Marcus's Parthian victory." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg/300px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg/450px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg/600px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_166%2C_RIC_III_160.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="380"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_166,_RIC_III_160.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aureus of Marcus Aurelius (AD 166). On the reverse, <a href="/wiki/Victoria_(mythology)" title="Victoria (mythology)">Victoria</a> is holding a shield inscribed 'VIC(toria) PAR(thica)', referring to his victory against the Parthians. Inscription: M. ANTONINVS AVG. / TR. P. XX, IMP. IIII, CO[N]S. III.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[216]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Marcus took a four-day public holiday at <a href="/wiki/Alsium" title="Alsium">Alsium</a>, a resort town on the coast of Etruria. He was too anxious to relax. Writing to Fronto, he declared that he would not speak about his holiday.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[217]</a></sup> Fronto replied: 'What? Do I not know that you went to Alsium with the intention of devoting yourself to games, joking, and complete leisure for four whole days?'<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[218]</a></sup> He encouraged Marcus to rest, calling on the example of his predecessors (Antoninus had enjoyed exercise in the <i><a href="/wiki/Palaestra" title="Palaestra">palaestra</a></i>, fishing, and comedy),<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[219]</a></sup> going so far as to write up a fable about the gods' division of the day between morning and evening – Marcus had apparently been spending most of his evenings on judicial matters instead of at leisure.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[220]</a></sup> Marcus could not take Fronto's advice. 'I have duties hanging over me that can hardly be begged off', he wrote back.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[221]</a></sup> Marcus Aurelius put on Fronto's voice to chastise himself: ''Much good has my advice done you', you will say!' He had rested, and would rest often, but 'this devotion to duty! Who knows better than you how demanding it is!'<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[222]</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:492px;max-width:492px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:174px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Depiction of Antioch, Syria" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg/222px-Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg" decoding="async" width="222" height="174" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg/333px-Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg/444px-Antioch_in_Syria_engraving_by_William_Miller_after_H_Warren.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4582" data-file-height="3596"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The dissolute Syrian army spent more time in <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>'s open-air taverns than with their units<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[223]</a></sup> (engraving by <a href="/wiki/William_Miller_(engraver)" title="William Miller (engraver)">William Miller</a> after a drawing by H. Warren from a sketch by Captain <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Byam_Martin" title="Thomas Byam Martin">Byam Martin</a>, R.N., 1866).</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:264px;max-width:264px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:174px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Euphrates River" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg/262px-ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg" decoding="async" width="262" height="175" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg/393px-ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg/524px-ArRaqqahEuphrates.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="840"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The Euphrates River near <a href="/wiki/Raqqa" title="Raqqa">Raqqa</a>, Syria</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Fronto sent Marcus a selection of reading material,<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[224]</a></sup> and, to settle his unease over the course of the Parthian war, a long and considered letter, full of historical references. In modern editions of Fronto's works, it is labeled <i>De bello Parthico</i> (<i>On the Parthian War</i>). There had been reverses in Rome's past, Fronto writes,<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[225]</a></sup> but in the end, Romans had always prevailed over their enemies: 'Always and everywhere [Mars] has changed our troubles into successes and our terrors into triumphs'.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[226]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over the winter of 161–162, news that a rebellion was brewing in Syria arrived and it was decided that Lucius should direct the Parthian war in person. He was stronger and healthier than Marcus, the argument went, and thus more suited to military activity.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[227]</a></sup> Lucius's biographer suggests ulterior motives: to restrain Lucius's debaucheries, to make him thrifty, to reform his morals by the terror of war, and to realize that he was an emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[228]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[note 15]</a></sup> Whatever the case, the senate gave its assent, and, in the summer of 162, Lucius left. Marcus would remain in Rome, as the city 'demanded the presence of an emperor'.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>Lucius spent most of the campaign in Antioch, though he wintered at <a href="/wiki/Laodicea_in_Syria" title="Laodicea in Syria">Laodicea</a> and summered at Daphne, a resort just outside Antioch.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[231]</a></sup> Critics declaimed Lucius's luxurious lifestyle,<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[232]</a></sup> saying that he had taken to gambling, would 'dice the whole night through',<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[233]</a></sup> and enjoyed the company of actors.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[234]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[note 16]</a></sup> Libo died early in the war; perhaps Lucius had murdered him.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[236]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Statue of Marcus's daughter Lucilla" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1824" data-file-height="2008"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Lucilla_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marble statue of <a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Lucilla</a>, AD 150–200, <a href="/wiki/Bardo_National_Museum_(Tunis)" title="Bardo National Museum (Tunis)">Bardo National Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the middle of the war, perhaps in autumn 163 or early 164, Lucius made a trip to <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a> to be married to Marcus's daughter Lucilla.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[237]</a></sup> Marcus moved up the date; perhaps he had already heard of Lucius's mistress Panthea.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[238]</a></sup> Lucilla's thirteenth birthday was in March 163; whatever the date of her marriage, she was not yet fifteen.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[239]</a></sup> Lucilla was accompanied by her mother Faustina and Lucius's uncle (his father's half-brother) M. Vettulenus Civica Barbarus,<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[240]</a></sup> who was made <i><a href="/wiki/Comes" title="Comes">comes</a> Augusti</i>, 'companion of the emperors'. Marcus may have wanted Civica to watch over Lucius, the job Libo had failed at.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[241]</a></sup> Marcus may have planned to accompany them all the way to Smyrna (the biographer says he told the senate he would), but this did not happen.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[242]</a></sup> He only accompanied the group as far as <a href="/wiki/Brundisium" class="mw-redirect" title="Brundisium">Brundisium</a>, where they boarded a ship for the east.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[243]</a></sup> He returned to Rome immediately thereafter, and sent out special instructions to his proconsuls not to give the group any official reception.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Armenian capital <a href="/wiki/Artashat_(ancient_city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Artashat (ancient city)">Artaxata</a> was captured in 163.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[245]</a></sup> At the end of the year, Lucius took the title <i>Armeniacus</i>, despite having never seen combat; Marcus declined to accept the title until the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[246]</a></sup> When Lucius was hailed as <i>imperator</i> again, however, Marcus did not hesitate to take the <i>Imperator II</i> with him.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>Occupied Armenia was reconstructed on Roman terms. In 164, a new capital, Kaine Polis ('New City'), replaced Artaxata.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[248]</a></sup> A new king was installed: a Roman senator of consular rank and Arsacid descent, <a href="/wiki/Sohaemus_of_Armenia" title="Sohaemus of Armenia">Gaius Julius Sohaemus</a>. He may not even have been crowned in Armenia; the ceremony may have taken place in Antioch, or even Ephesus.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[249]</a></sup> Sohaemus was hailed on the imperial coinage of 164 under the legend <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r920966791">.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps{font-variant:small-caps}.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps-smaller{font-size:85%}</style><span class="smallcaps">Rex armeniis Datus</span>: Lucius sat on a throne with his staff while Sohaemus stood before him, saluting the emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[250]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 163, the Parthians intervened in <a href="/wiki/Osroene" title="Osroene">Osroene</a>, a Roman client in upper Mesopotamia centred on <a href="/wiki/Edessa,_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Edessa, Mesopotamia">Edessa</a>, and installed their own king on its throne.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[251]</a></sup> In response, Roman forces were moved downstream, to cross the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> at a more southerly point.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[252]</a></sup> Before the end of 163, however, Roman forces had moved north to occupy Dausara and Nicephorium on the northern, Parthian bank.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[253]</a></sup> Soon after the conquest of the north bank of the Euphrates, other Roman forces moved on Osroene from Armenia, taking Anthemusia, a town southwest of Edessa.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[254]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 165, Roman forces moved on Mesopotamia. Edessa was re-occupied, and Mannus, the king deposed by the Parthians, was re-installed.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[255]</a></sup> The Parthians retreated to Nisibis, but this too was besieged and captured. The Parthian army dispersed in the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[256]</a></sup> A second force, under Avidius Cassius and the III Gallica, moved down the Euphrates, and fought a major battle at Dura.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the year, Cassius's army had reached the twin metropolises of Mesopotamia: <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> on the right bank of the Tigris and <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> on the left. Ctesiphon was taken and its royal palace set to flame. The citizens of Seleucia, still largely Greek (the city had been commissioned and settled as a capital of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, one of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">successor kingdoms</a>), opened its gates to the invaders. The city was sacked nonetheless, leaving a black mark on Lucius's reputation. Excuses were sought, or invented: the official version had it that the Seleucids broke faith first.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[258]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cassius's army, although suffering from a shortage of supplies and the effects of a plague contracted in Seleucia, made it back to Roman territory safely.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[259]</a></sup> Lucius took the title Parthicus Maximus, and he and Marcus were hailed as <i>imperatores</i> again, earning the title 'imp. III'.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[260]</a></sup> Cassius's army returned to the field in 166, crossing over the Tigris into <a href="/wiki/Media_(region)" title="Media (region)">Media</a>. Lucius took the title 'Medicus',<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[261]</a></sup> and the emperors were again hailed as <i>imperatores</i>, becoming 'imp. IV' in imperial titulature. Marcus took the Parthicus Maximus now, after another tactful delay.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[262]</a></sup> On 12 October of that year, Marcus proclaimed two of his sons, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_Caesar" title="Marcus Annius Verus Caesar">Annius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">his heirs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-279">[263]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="War_with_Germanic_tribes_.28166.E2.80.93180.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="War_with_Germanic_tribes_(166–180)">War with Germanic tribes (166–180)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: War with Germanic tribes (166–180)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:363px;max-width:363px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Scenes from the <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a>, AD 176–180 (bas reliefs from the Arch of Marcus Aurelius, now in the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museums" title="Capitoline Museums">Capitoline Museums</a>)</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:0_Relief_-_Monument_honoraire_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_-_La_soumission_des_germains_(1).JPG" class="image"><img alt="Scene 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/0_Relief_-_Monument_honoraire_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_-_La_soumission_des_germains_%281%29.JPG/320px-0_Relief_-_Monument_honoraire_de_Marc_Aur%C3%A8le_-_La_soumission_des_germains_%281%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2699" data-file-height="3599"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Marcus Aurelius receiving the submission of the vanquished, with raised <i><a href="/wiki/Vexillum" title="Vexillum">vexillum</a></i> standards</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:197px;max-width:197px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Rilievo_da_monumento_onorario_di_Marco_Aurelio_trionfo,_176-180.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Scene from the Arch of Marcus Aurelius" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Rilievo_da_monumento_onorario_di_Marco_Aurelio_trionfo%2C_176-180.JPG/195px-Rilievo_da_monumento_onorario_di_Marco_Aurelio_trionfo%2C_176-180.JPG" decoding="async" width="195" height="212" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Rilievo_da_monumento_onorario_di_Marco_Aurelio_trionfo%2C_176-180.JPG/293px-Rilievo_da_monumento_onorario_di_Marco_Aurelio_trionfo%2C_176-180.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Rilievo_da_monumento_onorario_di_Marco_Aurelio_trionfo%2C_176-180.JPG/390px-Rilievo_da_monumento_onorario_di_Marco_Aurelio_trionfo%2C_176-180.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2088"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Marcus Aurelius celebrating <a href="/wiki/Roman_triumph" title="Roman triumph">his triumph</a> over Rome's enemies in AD 176, riding in a <i><a href="/wiki/Quadriga" title="Quadriga">quadriga</a></i> chariot</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>During the early 160s, Fronto's son-in-law Victorinus was stationed as a legate in Germany. He was there with his wife and children (another child had stayed with Fronto and his wife in Rome).<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[264]</a></sup> The condition on the northern frontier looked grave. A frontier post had been destroyed, and it looked like all the peoples of central and northern Europe were in turmoil. There was corruption among the officers: Victorinus had to ask for the resignation of a <a href="/wiki/Legionary" title="Legionary">legionary</a> legate who was taking bribes.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[265]</a></sup>
</p><p>Experienced governors had been replaced by friends and relatives of the imperial family. <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Dasumius_Tullius_Tuscus" title="Lucius Dasumius Tullius Tuscus">Lucius Dasumius Tullius Tuscus</a>, a distant relative of Hadrian, was in Upper Pannonia, succeeding the experienced <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Nonius_Macrinus" title="Marcus Nonius Macrinus">Marcus Nonius Macrinus</a>. Lower Pannonia was under the obscure <a href="/w/index.php?title=Tiberius_Haterius_Saturnius&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Tiberius Haterius Saturnius (page does not exist)">Tiberius Haterius Saturnius</a>. <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Servilius_Fabianus_Maximus" title="Marcus Servilius Fabianus Maximus">Marcus Servilius Fabianus Maximus</a> was shuffled from Lower Moesia to Upper Moesia when <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Iallius_Bassus" title="Marcus Iallius Bassus">Marcus Iallius Bassus</a> had joined Lucius in Antioch. Lower Moesia was filled by Pontius Laelianus's son. The Dacias were still divided in three, governed by a praetorian senator and two procurators. The peace could not hold long; Lower Pannonia did not even have a legion.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting in the 160s, <a href="/wiki/Germanic_tribes" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic tribes">Germanic tribes</a>, and other nomadic people launched raids along the <a href="/wiki/Limes_Germanicus" title="Limes Germanicus">northern border</a>, particularly into <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> and across the <a href="/wiki/Danube#History" title="Danube">Danube</a>. This new impetus westwards was probably due to attacks from tribes further east. A first invasion by the Chatti in the province of Germania Superior was repulsed in 162.<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn2010_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn2010-283">[267]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_AE_medallion,_AD_168,_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Bust of Marcus Aurelius." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_AE_medallion%2C_AD_168%2C_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg/300px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_AE_medallion%2C_AD_168%2C_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_AE_medallion%2C_AD_168%2C_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg/450px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_AE_medallion%2C_AD_168%2C_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d8/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_AE_medallion%2C_AD_168%2C_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="236"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_AE_medallion,_AD_168,_Gnecchi_II_52.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bronze medallion of Marcus Aurelius (AD 168). The reverse depicts <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>, flanked by Marcus and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>. Inscription: M. ANTONINVS AVG. ARM. PARTH. MAX. / TR. P. XXII, IMP. IIII, COS III.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[268]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_161-180,_RIC_III_362.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Aureus of Marcus Aurelius." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg/300px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg/450px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg/600px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_161-180%2C_RIC_III_362.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="399"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_161-180,_RIC_III_362.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">Aureus</a> of Marcus Aurelius (AD 176–177). The pile of trophies on the reverse celebrates the end of the Marcomannic Wars. Inscription: M. ANTONINVS AVG. GERM. SARM. / TR. P. XXXI, IMP. VIII, CO[N]S. III, P. P.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[269]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Far more dangerous was the invasion of 166, when the <a href="/wiki/Marcomanni" title="Marcomanni">Marcomanni</a> of Bohemia, clients of the Roman Empire since AD 19, crossed the Danube together with the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> and other Germanic tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-Bohec2013_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bohec2013-286">[270]</a></sup> Soon thereafter, the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatian</a> <a href="/wiki/Iazyges" title="Iazyges">Iazyges</a> attacked between the Danube and the <a href="/wiki/Tisza" title="Tisza">Theiss</a> rivers.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant2016_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2016-287">[271]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Costoboci" title="Costoboci">Costoboci</a>, coming from the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian</a> area, invaded <a href="/wiki/Moesia" title="Moesia">Moesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Roman_province)" title="Macedonia (Roman province)">Macedonia</a>, and Greece. After a long struggle, Marcus managed to push back the invaders. Numerous members of Germanic tribes settled in frontier regions like <a href="/wiki/Dacia" title="Dacia">Dacia</a>, Pannonia, Germany, and Italy itself. This was not a new thing, but this time the numbers of settlers required the creation of two new frontier provinces on the left shore of the Danube, Sarmatia and <a href="/wiki/Marcomannia" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcomannia">Marcomannia</a>, including today's <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>. Some Germanic tribes who settled in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> revolted and managed to seize possession of the city. For this reason, Marcus decided not only against bringing more barbarians into Italy, but even banished those who had previously been brought there.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[272]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_and_administrative_work">Legal and administrative work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legal and administrative work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like many emperors, Marcus spent most of his time addressing matters of law such as petitions and hearing disputes,<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[273]</a></sup> but unlike many of his predecessors, he was already proficient in imperial administration when he assumed power.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[274]</a></sup> He took great care in the theory and practice of legislation. Professional jurists called him "an emperor most skilled in the law"<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[275]</a></sup> and "a most prudent and conscientiously just emperor".<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[276]</a></sup> He showed marked interest in three areas of the law: the <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">manumission</a> of slaves, the guardianship of orphans and minors, and the choice of city councillors (<i>decuriones</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marcus showed a great deal of respect to the Roman Senate and routinely asked them for permission to spend money even though he did not need to do so as the absolute ruler of the Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[278]</a></sup> In one speech, Marcus himself reminded the Senate that the imperial palace where he lived was not truly his possession but theirs.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[279]</a></sup> In 168, he revalued the denarius, increasing the silver purity from 79% to 82% – the actual silver weight increasing from 2.57–2.67&nbsp;g (0.091–0.094&nbsp;oz). However, two years later he reverted to the previous values because of the military crises facing the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-TulaneUniversity_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TulaneUniversity-177">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_with_Han_China_and_outbreak_of_plague">Trade with Han China and outbreak of plague</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Trade with Han China and outbreak of plague">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Sino-Roman relations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">Antonine Plague</a></div>
<p>A possible contact with <a href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Han Dynasty">Han China</a> occurred in 166 when a <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Roman traveller visited the Han court</a>, claiming to be an ambassador representing a certain Andun (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%89" class="extiw" title="wikt:安">安</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%95%A6" class="extiw" title="wikt:敦">敦</a>), ruler of <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a>, who can be identified either with Marcus or his predecessor Antoninus.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[280]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[281]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[282]</a></sup> In addition to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republican</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glasswares</a> found at <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> along the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[283]</a></sup> Roman golden medallions made during the reign of Antoninus and perhaps even Marcus have been found at <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="Óc Eo">Óc Eo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, then part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Funan">Kingdom of Funan</a> near the Chinese province of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> (in northern Vietnam). This may have been the port city of <a href="/wiki/Kattigara" class="mw-redirect" title="Kattigara">Kattigara</a>, described by <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (c. 150) as being visited by a Greek sailor named Alexander and lying beyond the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Chersonese" title="Golden Chersonese">Golden Chersonese</a> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-young_2001_pp._29-30_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_pp._29-30-300">[284]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[note 17]</a></sup> Roman coins from the reigns of Tiberius to <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> have been found in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>, China (site of the Han capital <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a>), although the far greater amount of <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">Roman coins in India</a> suggests the Roman maritime trade for <a href="/wiki/History_of_silk" title="History of silk">purchasing Chinese silk</a> was centred there, not in China or even the overland <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> running through Persia.<sup id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-302">[285]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">Antonine Plague</a> started in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> in 165 or 166 at the end of Lucius's campaign against the Parthians. It may have continued into the reign of <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, who was in Rome when the plague spread to the city in 166,<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-303">[286]</a></sup> mentioned that "fever, diarrhoea, and inflammation of the pharynx, along with dry or pustular eruptions of the skin after nine days" were among the symptoms.<sup id="cite_ref-murphy_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-murphy-304">[287]</a></sup> It is believed that the plague was <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_303-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-303">[286]</a></sup> In the view of historian <a href="/wiki/Rafe_de_Crespigny" title="Rafe de Crespigny">Rafe de Crespigny</a>, the plagues afflicting the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> empire of China during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huan_of_Han" title="Emperor Huan of Han">Emperor Huan of Han</a> (r. 146–168) and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ling_of_Han" title="Emperor Ling of Han">Emperor Ling of Han</a> (r. 168–189), which struck in 151, 161, 171, 173, 179, 182, and 185, were perhaps connected to the plague in Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[288]</a></sup> Raoul McLaughlin writes that the travel of Roman subjects to the Han Chinese court in 166 may have started a new era of Roman–Far East trade. However, it was also a "harbinger of something much more ominous". According to McLaughlin, the disease caused "irreparable" damage to the Roman maritime trade in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> as proven by the archaeological record spanning from <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Egypt</a> to <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_relations" title="Indo-Roman relations">India</a>, as well as significantly decreased <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">Roman commercial</a> activity in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mclaughlin_2010_p59-60_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mclaughlin_2010_p59-60-306">[289]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Death_and_succession_.28180.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession_(180)">Death and succession (180)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Death and succession (180)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting that depicts Marcus on his deathbed and his son Commodus, surrounded by the emperor's philosopher friends" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg/220px-Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg/330px-Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg/440px-Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1173"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Delacroix-Marc_Aur%C3%A8le-MBA-Lyon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Last_Words_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Last Words of the Emperor Marcus Aurelius">Last Words of the Emperor Marcus Aurelius</a></i> (1844) by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a></div></div></div>
<p>Marcus Aurelius died at the age of 58 on 17 March 180<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[290]</a></sup> of unknown causes in his military quarters either in the city of <a href="/wiki/Vindobona" title="Vindobona">Vindobona</a> (province of <a href="/wiki/Pannonia_Superior" title="Pannonia Superior">Pannonia Superior</a>, today <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>) or near of <a href="/wiki/Sirmium" title="Sirmium">Sirmium</a> (province of <a href="/wiki/Pannonia_Inferior" title="Pannonia Inferior">Pannonia Inferior</a>, modern <a href="/wiki/Sremska_Mitrovica" title="Sremska Mitrovica">Sremska Mitrovica</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[note 18]</a></sup> He was immediately deified and <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">his ashes</a> were returned to Rome, where they rested in Hadrian's <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum" title="Mausoleum">mausoleum</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Castel_Sant%27Angelo" title="Castel Sant'Angelo">Castel Sant'Angelo</a>) until the <a href="/wiki/Visigoth" class="mw-redirect" title="Visigoth">Visigoth</a> <a href="/wiki/Sack_of_Rome_(410)" title="Sack of Rome (410)">sack of the city</a> in 410. His campaigns against Germans and Sarmatians were also commemorated by a <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">column</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Temple of Marcus Aurelius">temple</a> built in Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-Kleiner2016_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kleiner2016-309">[291]</a></sup> Some scholars consider his death to be the end of the <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Romana</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Merrony2017_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merrony2017-310">[292]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aurelius180AD.png" class="image"><img alt="Expanse of the Roman Empire during Marcus Aurelius's reign" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Aurelius180AD.png/300px-Aurelius180AD.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Aurelius180AD.png/450px-Aurelius180AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Aurelius180AD.png/600px-Aurelius180AD.png 2x" data-file-width="747" data-file-height="493"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aurelius180AD.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> at the death of Marcus Aurelius in 180, represented in purple. His annexation of lands of the Marcomanni and the Jazyges – perhaps to be provincially called <a href="/wiki/Marcomannia" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcomannia">Marcomannia</a> and Sarmatia<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[293]</a></sup> – was cut short in 175 by the revolt of <a href="/wiki/Avidius_Cassius" title="Avidius Cassius">Avidius Cassius</a> and by his death.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[294]</a></sup> The light pink territory represents Roman dependencies: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colchis" title="Colchis">Colchis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iberia" title="Kingdom of Iberia">Iberia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_Albania" title="Caucasian Albania">Albania</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Marcus was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>, whom he had named Caesar in 166 and with whom he had jointly ruled since 177.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley186–91_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley186–91-313">[295]</a></sup> Biological sons of the emperor, if there were any, were considered heirs;<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[296]</a></sup> however, it was only the second time that a "non-adoptive" son had succeeded his father, the only other having been a century earlier when <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a> was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Titus" title="Titus">Titus</a>. Historians have criticized the succession to Commodus, citing Commodus's erratic behaviour and lack of political and military acumen.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley186–91_313-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley186–91-313">[295]</a></sup> At the end of his history of Marcus's reign, Cassius Dio wrote an <a href="/wiki/Encomium" title="Encomium">encomium</a> to the emperor, and described the transition to Commodus in his own lifetime with sorrow:<sup id="cite_ref-Tr._Cary,_ad_loc_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tr._Cary,_ad_loc-315">[297]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>[Marcus] did not meet with the good fortune that he deserved, for he was not strong in body and was involved in a multitude of troubles throughout practically his entire reign. But for my part, I admire him all the more for this very reason, that amid unusual and extraordinary difficulties he both survived himself and preserved the empire. Just one thing prevented him from being completely happy, namely, that after rearing and educating his son in the best possible way he was vastly disappointed in him. This matter must be our next topic; for our history now descends from a kingdom of gold to one of iron and rust, as affairs did for the Romans of that day.
</p><dl><dd>–Dio lxxi. 36.3–4<sup id="cite_ref-Tr._Cary,_ad_loc_315-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tr._Cary,_ad_loc-315">[297]</a></sup></dd></dl></blockquote>
<p>Dio adds that from Marcus's first days as counsellor to Antoninus to his final days as emperor of Rome, "he remained the same [person] and did not change in the least."<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Grant_(classicist)" title="Michael Grant (classicist)">Michael Grant</a>, in <i>The Climax of Rome</i>, writes of Commodus:<sup id="cite_ref-Grant2011_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2011-317">[299]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>The youth turned out to be very erratic, or at least so anti-traditional that disaster was inevitable. But whether or not Marcus ought to have known this to be so, the rejections of his son's claims in favour of someone else would almost certainly have involved one of the civil wars which were to proliferate so disastrously around future successions.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant2011_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant2011-317">[299]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitude_towards_Christians">Attitude towards Christians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Attitude towards Christians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the first two centuries of the Christian era, it was local Roman officials who were largely responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Persecution of Christians in the Roman Empire">persecution of Christians</a>. In the second century, the emperors treated Christianity as a local problem to be dealt with by their subordinates.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[300]</a></sup> The number and severity of persecutions of Christians in various locations of the empire seemingly increased during the reign of Marcus Aurelius. The extent to which the emperor himself directed, encouraged, or was aware of these persecutions is unclear and much debated by historians.<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn2010295_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn2010295-319">[301]</a></sup> The early Christian apologist Justin Martyr includes within his First Apology (written between AD 140 and 150) a letter from Marcus Aurelius to the Roman Senate (prior to his reign) describing a battlefield incident in which Marcus believed Christian prayer had saved his army from thirst when "water poured from heaven" after which, "immediately we recognized the presence of God." Marcus goes on to request the Senate desist from earlier courses of Christian persecution by Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[302]</a></sup> However, this letter was one of three from Roman emperors included by Martyr, two of which (including the Aurelius letter) are regarded as spurious.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[303]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_children"><span class="anchor" id="Marriage_and_issue"></span>Marriage and children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Marriage and children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:164px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Commodus-AnniusVerus_tarsos_161-165_AE17_CNG.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Coin of Marcus's sons Commodus and Annius facing each other" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Commodus-AnniusVerus_tarsos_161-165_AE17_CNG.jpg/162px-Commodus-AnniusVerus_tarsos_161-165_AE17_CNG.jpg" decoding="async" width="162" height="164" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Commodus-AnniusVerus_tarsos_161-165_AE17_CNG.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="196" data-file-height="198"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Coin of <a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_Caesar" title="Marcus Annius Verus Caesar">Annius</a>, 161–165. Inscription: [ΝΕΩ]ΚΟΡΟΙ CΕΒΑCΤΟΥ i.e. the city (of Tarsus in Cilicia) had a temple of Augustus.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:124px;max-width:124px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:164px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_(M._Prado)_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Bust of Vibia Aurelia Sabina, Marcus's daughter" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg/122px-Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="122" height="165" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg/183px-Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg/244px-Busto_de_Vibia_Sabina_%28M._Prado%29_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1090" data-file-height="1470"></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Bust of <a href="/wiki/Vibia_Aurelia_Sabina" title="Vibia Aurelia Sabina">Vibia Aurelia Sabina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Museo_del_Prado" title="Museo del Prado">Prado Museum</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Marcus and his <a href="/wiki/Cousin_marriage" title="Cousin marriage">cousin-wife</a> <a href="/wiki/Faustina_the_Younger" title="Faustina the Younger">Faustina</a> had at least 14 children<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[304]</a></sup> during their 30-year marriage,<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-135">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stephens,_p._31_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens,_p._31-323">[305]</a></sup> including two sets of twins.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-135">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-livius3_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius3-324">[306]</a></sup> One son and four daughters outlived their father.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[307]</a></sup> Their children included:
</p>
<ul><li>Domitia Faustina (147–151)<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._103-135">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92-326">[308]</a></sup></li>
<li>Titus Aelius Antoninus (149)<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._106–07_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._106–07-138">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-livius3_324-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius3-324">[306]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-levick171_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-levick171-327">[309]</a></sup></li>
<li>Titus Aelius Aurelius (149)<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._106–07_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_pp._106–07-138">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-livius3_324-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius3-324">[306]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-levick171_327-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-levick171-327">[309]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lucilla" title="Lucilla">Annia Aurelia Galeria Lucilla</a> (150<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_107_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_107-141">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92_326-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92-326">[308]</a></sup>–182<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[310]</a></sup>), married her father's co-ruler <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup> then <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Claudius_Pompeianus" title="Tiberius Claudius Pompeianus">Tiberius Claudius Pompeianus</a>, had issue from both marriages</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Annia_Galeria_Aurelia_Faustina" title="Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina">Annia Galeria Aurelia Faustina</a> (born 151),<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108-143">[136]</a></sup> married <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Claudius_Severus_(consul_167)" title="Gnaeus Claudius Severus (consul 167)">Gnaeus Claudius Severus</a>, had a son</li>
<li>Tiberius Aelius Antoninus (born 152, died before 156)<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108_143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._108-143">[136]</a></sup></li>
<li>Unknown child (died before 158)<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114-145">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fadilla" title="Fadilla">Annia Aurelia Fadilla</a> (born 159<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92_326-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92-326">[308]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114_145-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114-145">[138]</a></sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup> married <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Peducaeus_Plautius_Quintillus" title="Marcus Peducaeus Plautius Quintillus">Marcus Peducaeus Plautius Quintillus</a>, had issue</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Annia_Cornificia_Faustina_Minor" title="Annia Cornificia Faustina Minor">Annia Cornificia Faustina Minor</a> (born 160<sup id="cite_ref-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92_326-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McLynn,_A_Life,_p._92-326">[308]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114_145-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._114-145">[138]</a></sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup> married <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Petronius_Sura_Mamertinus" title="Marcus Petronius Sura Mamertinus">Marcus Petronius Sura Mamertinus</a>, had a son</li>
<li>Titus Aurelius Fulvus Antoninus (161–165), elder twin brother of Commodus<sup id="cite_ref-levick171_327-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-levick171-327">[309]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lucius_Aurelius_Commodus_Antoninus" class="mw-redirect" title="Lucius Aurelius Commodus Antoninus">Lucius Aurelius Commodus Antoninus</a> (Commodus) (161–192),<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[311]</a></sup> twin brother of Titus Aurelius Fulvus Antoninus, later emperor,<sup id="cite_ref-levick171_327-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-levick171-327">[309]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[312]</a></sup> married <a href="/wiki/Bruttia_Crispina" title="Bruttia Crispina">Bruttia Crispina</a>, no issue</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marcus_Annius_Verus_Caesar" title="Marcus Annius Verus Caesar">Marcus Annius Verus Caesar</a> (162<sup id="cite_ref-auto_279-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-279">[263]</a></sup>–169<sup id="cite_ref-Stephens,_p._31_323-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens,_p._31-323">[305]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">[313]</a></sup>)<sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li>Hadrianus<sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vibia_Aurelia_Sabina" title="Vibia Aurelia Sabina">Vibia Aurelia Sabina</a> (170<sup id="cite_ref-levick171_327-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-levick171-327">[309]</a></sup>– died before 217<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[314]</a></sup>),<sup id="cite_ref-livius1_147-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livius1-147">[140]</a></sup> married <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Antistius_Burrus" title="Lucius Antistius Burrus">Lucius Antistius Burrus</a>, no issue</li></ul>

<li>dashed lines indicate adoption; dotted lines indicate love affairs/unmarried relationships</li>
