<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg/220px-MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg/330px-MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg/440px-MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg 2x" data-file-width="783" data-file-height="642"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MeditationsMarcusAurelius1811.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of the 1811 English translation by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Graves" title="Richard Graves">Richard Graves</a></div></div></div>
<p>While on campaign between 170 and 180, Marcus wrote his <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i> in Greek as a source for his own guidance and self-improvement. The original title of this work, if it had one, is unknown. 'Meditations' – as well as other titles including 'To Himself' – were adopted later. He had a logical mind, and his notes were representative of Stoic philosophy and spirituality. <i>Meditations</i> is still revered as a literary monument to a government of service and duty.  <a href="/wiki/George_Long_(scholar)" title="George Long (scholar)">George Long</a>'s English translation of <i>Meditations</i> was included in Volume 2 of the <i><a href="/wiki/Harvard_Classics" title="Harvard Classics">Harvard Classics</a></i>.  According to Hays, the book was a favourite of <a href="/wiki/Christina_of_Sweden" class="mw-redirect" title="Christina of Sweden">Christina of Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_the_Great" title="Frederick the Great">Frederick the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Arnold" title="Matthew Arnold">Matthew Arnold</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Goethe" class="mw-redirect" title="Goethe">Goethe</a>, and is admired by modern figures such as <a href="/wiki/Wen_Jiabao" title="Wen Jiabao">Wen Jiabao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">[315]</a></sup> It has been considered by many commentators to be one of the greatest works of philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-Collins1973_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collins1973-358">[316]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is not known how widely Marcus's writings were circulated after his death. There are stray references in the ancient literature to the popularity of his precepts, and <a href="/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate" class="mw-redirect" title="Julian the Apostate">Julian the Apostate</a> was well aware of his reputation as a philosopher, though he does not specifically mention <i>Meditations</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">[317]</a></sup> It survived in the scholarly traditions of the Eastern Church, and the first surviving quotes of the book, as well as the first known reference of it by name ('Marcus's writings to himself') are from <a href="/wiki/Arethas_of_Caesarea" title="Arethas of Caesarea">Arethas of Caesarea</a> in the 10th century and in the Byzantine <a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a> (perhaps inserted by Arethas himself). It was first published in 1558 in Zurich by <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Xylander" title="Wilhelm Xylander">Wilhelm Xylander (né Holzmann)</a>, from a manuscript reportedly lost shortly afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">[318]</a></sup> The oldest surviving complete manuscript copy is in the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_library" class="mw-redirect" title="Vatican library">Vatican library</a> and dates to the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">[319]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_174,_RIC_III_295.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Aureus of Marcus Aurelius" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg/220px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg/330px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg/440px-Marcus_Aurelius%2C_aureus%2C_AD_174%2C_RIC_III_295.jpg 2x" data-file-width="736" data-file-height="343"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marcus_Aurelius,_aureus,_AD_174,_RIC_III_295.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Aureus" title="Aureus">Aureus</a> of Marcus Aurelius (AD December 173 – June 174), with his equestrian statue on the reverse. inscription: M. ANTONINVS AVG. TR. P. XXVIII / IMP. VI, CO[N]S III.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">[320]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</a> in Rome is the only Roman equestrian statue which has survived into the modern period.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">[321]</a></sup> This may be due to it being wrongly identified during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> as a depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">Christian emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a>, and spared the destruction which statues of <a href="/wiki/Pagan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagan">pagan</a> figures suffered.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_(176_dC),_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_(Roma)._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_%28176_dC%29%2C_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_%28Roma%29._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg/220px-Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_%28176_dC%29%2C_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_%28Roma%29._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_%28176_dC%29%2C_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_%28Roma%29._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg/330px-Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_%28176_dC%29%2C_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_%28Roma%29._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_%28176_dC%29%2C_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_%28Roma%29._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg/440px-Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_%28176_dC%29%2C_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_%28Roma%29._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4134" data-file-height="2558"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Est%C3%A0tua_eq%C3%BCestre_de_Marc_Aureli_(176_dC),_Museu_Capitol%C3%AD_(Roma)._Vista_general_de_la_sala_d%27exposici%C3%B3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The original <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_Statue_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius">Equestrian Statue of Marcus Aurelius</a>, 2nd century AD, now located in the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_dei_Conservatori" class="mw-redirect" title="Palazzo dei Conservatori">Palazzo dei Conservatori</a></div></div></div>
<p>Crafted of bronze in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 175</span>, it stands 11.6&nbsp;ft (3.5&nbsp;m) and is now located in the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museums" title="Capitoline Museums">Capitoline Museums</a> of Rome. The emperor's hand is outstretched in an act of clemency offered to a bested enemy, while his weary facial expression due to the stress of leading Rome into nearly constant battles perhaps represents a break with the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_art" title="Ancient Greek art">classical tradition</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_sculpture" title="Ancient Greek sculpture">sculpture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">[322]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius">Column of Marcus Aurelius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Column of Marcus Aurelius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Marcus_Aurelius" title="Column of Marcus Aurelius">Column of Marcus Aurelius</a></div>
<p>Marcus's <a href="/wiki/Victory_column" title="Victory column">victory column</a>, established in Rome either in his last few years of life or after his reign and completed in 193, was built to commemorate his victory over the Sarmatians and Germanic tribes in 176. A spiral of carved <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a> wraps around the column, showing scenes from his military campaigns. A statue of Marcus had stood atop the column but disappeared during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. It was replaced with a statue of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Paul">Saint Paul</a> in 1589 by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_V" title="Pope Sixtus V">Pope Sixtus V</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">[323]</a></sup> The column of Marcus and the <a href="/wiki/Column_of_Trajan" class="mw-redirect" title="Column of Trajan">column of Trajan</a> are often compared by scholars given how they are both <a href="/wiki/Doric_order" title="Doric order">Doric</a> in style, had a pedestal at the base, had sculpted <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">friezes</a> depicting their respective military victories, and a statue on top.<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">[324]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_reputation">Legacy and reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Legacy and reputation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1136" data-file-height="1371"></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Portrait_of_the_Emperor_Marcus_Aurelius_-_Walters_23215_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 2nd century <a href="/wiki/Roman_portraiture" title="Roman portraiture">Roman sculpted portrait</a> of Marcus Aurelius, which captures the pensive temperament of the philosopher-emperor, now in the <a href="/wiki/Walters_Art_Museum" title="Walters Art Museum">Walters Art Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Marcus acquired the reputation of a <a href="/wiki/Philosopher_king" title="Philosopher king">philosopher king</a> within his lifetime, and the title would remain after his death; both Dio and the biographer call him "the philosopher".<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">[325]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">[326]</a></sup> Christians such as <a href="/wiki/Justin_Martyr" title="Justin Martyr">Justin Martyr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Athenagoras_of_Athens" title="Athenagoras of Athens">Athenagoras</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a> also gave him the title.<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">[327]</a></sup> The latter went so far as to call him "more philanthropic and philosophic" than Antoninus and Hadrian, and set him against the persecuting emperors Domitian and Nero to make the contrast bolder.<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">[328]</a></sup>
</p><p>
The historian Herodian wrote:</p><blockquote><p>Alone of the emperors, he gave proof of his learning not by mere words or knowledge of philosophical doctrines but by his blameless character and temperate way of life.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">[329]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">Iain King</a> explains that Marcus's legacy was tragic: </p><blockquote><p>[The emperor's] Stoic philosophy – which is about self-restraint, duty, and respect for others – was so abjectly abandoned by the imperial line he anointed on his death.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">[330]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Dilip_Kumar" title="Dilip Kumar">Dilip Kumar</a> played a fictionalized version of Marcus Aurelius in the 1958 Hindi movie <i><a href="/wiki/Yahudi" title="Yahudi">Yahudi</a></i></li>
<li>In the 1964 epic drama <i><a href="/wiki/The_Fall_of_the_Roman_Empire_(film)" title="The Fall of the Roman Empire (film)">The Fall of The Roman Empire</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Alec_Guinness" title="Alec Guinness">Alec Guinness</a> portrays Marcus Aurelius. The film is noteworthy for using quotes from <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Twin_Peaks" title="Twin Peaks">Twin Peaks</a></i>, the final episode of season 2 (1991), "<a href="/wiki/Episode_29_(Twin_Peaks)" title="Episode 29 (Twin Peaks)">Beyond Life and Death</a>", in the bank vault Marcus Aurelius is quoted (praising Audrey's civil disobedience): "Waste no time arguing what a good man should be. Be one."</li>
<li>In the 2000 film <i><a href="/wiki/Gladiator_(2000_film)" title="Gladiator (2000 film)">Gladiator</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Harris" title="Richard Harris">Richard Harris</a> portrays Marcus Aurelius as a mentor to the main character</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_emperors" title="List of Roman emperors">List of Roman emperors</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734"><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dio asserts that the Annii were near-kin of Hadrian, and that it was to these familial ties that they owed their rise to power.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The precise nature of these kinship ties is nowhere stated, but is believed that <a href="/wiki/Rupilia_Faustina" class="mw-redirect" title="Rupilia Faustina">Rupilia Faustina</a> was the daughter of the consular senator <a href="/wiki/Libo_Rupilius_Frugi" title="Libo Rupilius Frugi">Libo Rupilius Frugi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitellia_(daughter_of_emperor_Vitellius)" title="Vitellia (daughter of emperor Vitellius)">Vitellia</a>, daughter of emperor <a href="/wiki/Vitellius" title="Vitellius">Vitellius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Farquharson dates his death to 130 when Marcus was nine.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Birley amends the text of the <i>HA Marcus</i> from 'Eutychius' to 'Tuticius'.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Commodus was a known consumptive at the time of his adoption, so Hadrian may have intended Marcus's eventual succession anyway.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The manuscript is corrupt here.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._89-87">[83]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Modern scholars have not offered as positive an assessment. His second modern editor, <a href="/wiki/Barthold_Georg_Niebuhr" title="Barthold Georg Niebuhr">Niebhur</a>, thought him stupid and frivolous; his third editor, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Samuel_Adrian_Naber&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Samuel Adrian Naber (page does not exist)">Naber</a>, found him contemptible.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup> Historians have seen him as a 'pedant and a bore', his letters offering neither the running political analysis of a Cicero or the conscientious reportage of a Pliny.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup> Recent prosopographic research has rehabilitated his reputation, though not by much.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[102]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-129"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-129" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Champlin notes that Marcus's praise of Rusticus in the <i>Meditations</i> is out of order (he is praised immediately after Diognetus, who had introduced Marcus to philosophy), giving him special emphasis.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-152"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-152" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although part of the biographer's account of Lucius is fictionalized (probably to mimic Nero, whose birthday Lucius shared<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[142]</a></sup>) and another part poorly compiled from a better biographical source,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[143]</a></sup> scholars have accepted these biographical details as accurate.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[144]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-170" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These name-swaps have proven so confusing that even the <i>Historia Augusta</i>, our main source for the period, cannot keep them straight.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[159]</a></sup> The 4th-century ecclesiastical historian <a href="/wiki/Eusebius_of_Caesarea" class="mw-redirect" title="Eusebius of Caesarea">Eusebius of Caesarea</a> shows even more confusion.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[160]</a></sup> The mistaken belief that Lucius had the name 'Verus' before becoming emperor has proven especially popular.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-169">[161]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-171"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-171" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There was, however, much precedent. The consulate was a twin magistracy, and earlier emperors had often had a subordinate lieutenant with many imperial offices (under Antoninus, the lieutenant had been Marcus). Many emperors had planned a joint succession in the past: <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> planned to leave <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Caesar" title="Gaius Caesar">Gaius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Caesar" title="Lucius Caesar">Lucius Caesar</a> as joint emperors on his death; Tiberius wished to have <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Caligula" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaius Caligula">Gaius Caligula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Gemellus" title="Tiberius Gemellus">Tiberius Gemellus</a> do so as well; <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> left the empire to <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Britannicus" title="Britannicus">Britannicus</a>, imagining that they would accept equal rank. All of these arrangements had ended in failure, either through premature death (Gaius and Lucius Caesar) or judicial murder (Gemellus by Caligula and Britannicus by Nero).<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117_169-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._117-169">[161]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-185"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-185" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The biographer relates the scurrilous (and, in the judgment of Anthony Birley, untrue) rumor that Commodus was an illegitimate child born of a union between Faustina and a gladiator.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[174]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Because both Lucius and Marcus are said to have taken active part in the recovery (<i>HA Marcus</i> viii. 4–5), the flood must have happened before Lucius's departure for the east in 162; because it appears in the biographer's narrative after Antoninus's funeral has finished and the emperors have settled into their offices, it must not have occurred in the spring of 161. A date in autumn 161 or spring 162 is probable, and, given the normal seasonal distribution of Tiber flooding, the most probable date is in spring 162.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[190]</a></sup> (Birley dates the flood to autumn 161.<sup id="cite_ref-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120_196-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birley,_Marcus_Aurelius,_p._120-196">[185]</a></sup>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-205"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-205" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Since AD 15, the river had been administered by a Tiber Conservancy Board, with a consular senator at its head and a permanent staff. In 161, the <i>curator alevi Tiberis et riparum et cloacarum urbis</i> ('Curator of the Tiber Bed and Banks and the City Sewers') was A. Platorius Nepos, son or grandson of <a href="/wiki/Aulus_Platorius_Nepos" title="Aulus Platorius Nepos">the builder</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a>, whose name he shares. He probably had not been particularly incompetent. A more likely candidate for that incompetence is Nepos's likely predecessor, <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Statius_Priscus" title="Marcus Statius Priscus">M. Statius Priscus</a>. A military man and consul for 159, Priscus probably looked on the office as little more than 'paid leave'.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[192]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-221" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Alan_Cameron_(classical_scholar)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alan Cameron (classical scholar)">Alan Cameron</a> adduces the 5th-century writer <a href="/wiki/Sidonius_Apollinaris" title="Sidonius Apollinaris">Sidonius Apollinaris</a>'s comment that Marcus commanded 'countless legions' <i>vivente Pio</i> (while Antoninus was alive) while contesting Birley's contention that Marcus had no military experience. (Neither Apollinaris nor the <i>Historia Augusta</i> (Birley's source) are particularly reliable on 2nd-century history.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[207]</a></sup>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-244"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-244" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Birley believes there is some truth in these considerations.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[229]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-251"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-251" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The whole section of the <i>vita</i> dealing with Lucius's debaucheries (<i>HA Verus</i> iv. 4–6.6), however, is an insertion into a narrative otherwise entirely cribbed from an earlier source. Most of the details are fabricated by the biographer himself, relying on nothing better than his own imagination.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[235]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-301"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-301" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For further information on <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="Óc Eo">Óc Eo</a>, see Osborne, Milton. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=uxF2kH04WKgC"><i>The Mekong: Turbulent Past, Uncertain Future</i></a>. Crows Nest: Allen &amp; Unwin, 2006, revised edition, first published in 2000. pp. 24–25. <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1741148930" title="Special:BookSources/978-1741148930">978-1741148930</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-308"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-308" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Vindobona as Marc Aurel's death place is mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Aurelius_Victor" title="Aurelius Victor">Aurelius Victor</a> in his <i>De Caesaribus</i> (16.14), Sirmium on the other hand in <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian's</a> <i>Apologeticum</i> (25)</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Citations">Citations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marcus_Aurelius&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Citations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>All citations to the <i>Historia Augusta</i> are to individual biographies, and are marked with a <i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>HA<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span></i>. Citations to the works of Fronto are cross-referenced to C.R. Haines's Loeb edition.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-290"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-290" aria-label="Jump up" title="Jump up">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.military-history.org/articles/thinkers-at-war-marcus-aurelius.htm">'Thinkers At War – Marcus Aurelius'</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.military-history.org"><i>Military History Monthly</i></a>, published 2014. (This is the conclusion of <a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">Iain King</a>'s biography of Marcus Aurelius.) 'Pius, one of longest-serving emperors, became infirm in his last years, so Marcus Aurelius gradually assumed the imperial duties. By the time he succeeded in AD 161, he was already well-practised in public administration.'</span>
</li>
